style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\x99r/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">\xc9\x99r</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-Exeter.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/En-uk-Exeter.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-Exeter.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>) is a cathedral city in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, England, with a population of 129,800 (mid-2016 EST). The city is on the <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">River Exe</a> about 36 miles (58&#160;km) northeast of <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a> and 65 miles (105&#160;km) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county town</a> of Devon, and the home of <a href="/wiki/Devon_County_Council" title="Devon County Council">Devon County Council</a>. It is also home to two of the constituent campuses of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Streatham_Campus" title="Streatham Campus">Streatham Campus</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Luke%27s_Campus" title="St Luke&#39;s Campus">St Lukes Campus</a>.\n</p><p>Exeter was the most south-westerly Roman fortified settlement in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britain</a>. Exeter became a religious centre during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and into the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor England">Tudor</a> times: <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Cathedral" title="Exeter Cathedral">Exeter Cathedral</a>, founded in the mid 11th century, became <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> during the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>. During the late 19th century, Exeter became an affluent centre for the <a href="/wiki/Wool_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Wool trade">wool trade</a>, although by the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> the city was in decline. After the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, much of the city centre was rebuilt and is now considered to be a centre for modern business and tourism in Devon and Cornwall.\n</p><p>The administrative area of Exeter has the status of a <a href="/wiki/Non-metropolitan_district" title="Non-metropolitan district">non-metropolitan district</a> under the administration of the County Council; a plan to grant the city <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary authority</a> status was scrapped under the <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">2010 coalition government</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Theatre"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Theatre</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Music"><span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Sources_and_further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Sources and further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The modern name of Exeter is a development of the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>Escanceaster</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-tcodoepn_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcodoepn-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Anglicization_of_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicization of names">anglicised</a> form of the river now known as the <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">Exe</a> and the Old English <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> <span class="nowrap">-<i>ceaster</i></span>, used to mark important fortresses or fortified towns. (The Welsh name for the city, <i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text">Caerwysg</i>, similarly means "<a href="/wiki/Caer" title="Caer">fortress</a> on the Exe".) The name "Exe" is a separate development of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Brittonic</a> name&#8212;meaning "water"<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> or, more exactly, "full of fish" (cf. <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>pysg</i>, <abbr title="Plural">pl.</abbr> "fish")<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>&#8212;that also appears in the <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/River_Axe_(Lyme_Bay)" title="River Axe (Lyme Bay)">Axe</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Esk,_North_Yorkshire" title="River Esk, North Yorkshire">Esk</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> <a href="/wiki/River_Usk" title="River Usk">Usk</a> (<a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">Wysg</i>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Exeter" title="Timeline of Exeter">Timeline of Exeter</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">British Iron Age</a></div>\n<p>Exeter began as settlements on a dry ridge ending in a spur overlooking a navigable <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">river</a> teeming with fish, with fertile land nearby. Although there have been no major <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">prehistoric</a> finds, these advantages suggest the site was occupied early.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">Coins</a> have been discovered from the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic kingdoms</a>, suggesting the existence of a settlement trading with the Mediterranean as early as <span class="nowrap">250 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Such early towns had been a feature of pre-Roman <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> as described by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentaries</a></i> and it is possible that they existed in <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a> as well.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg/170px-Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg/255px-Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg/340px-Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Door_through_the_city_walls_-_geograph.org.uk_-_809408.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portion of Exeter\'s city wall, formed of both Roman and medieval stones.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_times">Roman times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Roman times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Isca_Dumnoniorum" title="Isca Dumnoniorum">Isca Dumnoniorum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Augusta" title="Legio II Augusta">Legio II Augusta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Romans">Romans</a> established a 42-acre (17&#160;ha) \'playing-card\' shaped (rectangle with round corners and two short and two long sides) fort (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Castrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Castrum">castrum</a></i>) named <b><a href="/wiki/Isca_Dumnoniorum" title="Isca Dumnoniorum">Isca</a></b> around <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>&#160;55. The fort was the southwest terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a> (Route 15 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Antonine_Itinerary" title="Antonine Itinerary">Antonine Itinerary</a></i>) and served as the base of the <span class="nowrap">5&#8201;000-</span>man <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Augusta" title="Legio II Augusta">Second Augustan Legion</a> (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Legio II Augusta</i>) originally led by Vespasian, later Roman Emperor, for the next 20 years before they moved to <a href="/wiki/Caerleon" title="Caerleon">Caerleon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, which was also known as <a href="/wiki/Isca_Augusta" title="Isca Augusta">Isca</a>. To distinguish the two, the Romans also referred to Exeter as <span class="nowrap"><b>Isca Dumnoniorum</b></span>, "Watertown of the <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a>", and Caerleon as Isca Augusta. A small fort was also maintained at <a href="/wiki/Topsham,_Devon" title="Topsham, Devon">Topsham</a>; a supply depot on the route between the two was excavated at <span class="nowrap">St Loyes</span> on Topsham Road in 2010.\n</p><p>The presence of the fort built up an unplanned civilian community (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Vicus" title="Vicus">vicus</a></i> or <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Canabae" class="mw-redirect" title="Canabae">canabae</a></i>) of <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">natives</a> and the soldiers\' families, mostly to the northeast of the fort. This settlement served as the <a href="/wiki/Civitas" title="Civitas">tribal capital</a> (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">civitas</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a><sup id="cite_ref-bidy_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bidy-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and was listed as one of their four cities (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <i lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a href="/wiki/Poleis" class="mw-redirect" title="Poleis">poleis</a></i>) by <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" class="mw-redirect" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-RBOD_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RBOD-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> (it also appeared in the 7th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Ravenna_Cosmography" title="Ravenna Cosmography">Ravenna Cosmography</a></i>, where it appears as an apparently <a href="/wiki/Scribal_error" class="mw-redirect" title="Scribal error">confused</a> entry for <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Scadu Namorum</i><sup id="cite_ref-RBOID_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RBOID-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>). When the fortress was abandoned around the year 75, its grounds were converted to civilian purposes: its very large <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">bathhouse</a> was demolished to make way for a <a href="/wiki/Forum_(Roman)" title="Forum (Roman)">forum</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a>, and a smaller-scale bath was erected to the southeast.<sup id="cite_ref-bidy_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bidy-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This area was excavated in the 1970s,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> but could not be maintained for public view owing to its proximity to the <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Cathedral" title="Exeter Cathedral">present-day cathedral</a>. In January 2015, it was announced that Exeter Cathedral had launched a bid to restore the baths and open an underground centre for visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 2nd century, the ditch and rampart defences around the old fortress were replaced by a bank and wall enclosing a much larger area, some 92&#160;acres (37&#160;ha).<sup id="cite_ref-B59_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B59-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Although most of the visible structure is older, the course of the Roman wall was used for Exeter\'s subsequent city walls. Thus about 70% of the Roman wall remains, and most of its route can be traced on foot. The Devonian Isca seems to have been most prosperous in the first half of the 4th century: more than a thousand <a href="/wiki/Roman_coins" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman coins">Roman coins</a> have been found around the city and there is evidence for copper and bronze working, a stock-yard, and markets for the livestock, crops, and pottery produced in the surrounding countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The dating of the coins so far discovered, however, suggests a rapid decline: virtually none have been discovered dated after the year 380.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_times">Medieval times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">Sub-Roman Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon England">Saxon England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norman_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman England">Norman England</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Bishop_Ussher" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop Ussher">Bishop Ussher</a> identified the <a href="/wiki/Caer" title="Caer">Cair</a> <span class="nowrap">Pensa <i>vel</i> Coyt</span><sup id="cite_ref-mommy_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mommy-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> listed among the 28 <a href="/wiki/Civitas" title="Civitas">cities</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">Britain</a> by the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">History of the Britons</a></i> as Isca,<sup id="cite_ref-shusher_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shusher-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> although David Nash Ford read it as a reference to <a href="/wiki/Penselwood" title="Penselwood">Penselwood</a> and thought it more likely to be <a href="/wiki/Lindinis" title="Lindinis">Lindinis</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Ilchester" title="Ilchester">Ilchester</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-nashford_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nashford-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Nothing is certainly known of Exeter from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_withdrawal_from_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman withdrawal from Britain">Roman withdrawal from Britain</a> around the year 410 until the seventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS15_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS15-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> By that time, the city was held by the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Saxons</a>, who had arrived in Exeter after defeating the <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">British</a> <a href="/wiki/Dumnonia" title="Dumnonia">Dumnonians</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Peonnum" title="Battle of Peonnum">Peonnum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> in 658.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> It seems likely that the Saxons maintained a quarter of the city for the Britons under their own laws around present-day Bartholomew Street,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> which was known as "Britayne" Street until 1637 in memory of its former occupants.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Exeter was known to the Saxons as <b>Escanceaster</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-tcodoepn_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcodoepn-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In 876, it was <a href="/wiki/Viking_invasions_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking invasions of England">attacked and briefly captured</a> by <a href="/wiki/Danes_(ancient_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Danes (ancient people)">Danish</a> <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> drove them out the next summer.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS23_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS23-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Over the next few years, he elevated Exeter to one of the four <i><a href="/wiki/Burh" title="Burh">burhs</a></i> in Devon, rebuilding its walls on the Roman lines.<sup id="cite_ref-SEL17_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEL17-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> These permitted the city to fend off another attack and siege by the Danes in 893.<sup id="cite_ref-SEL17_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEL17-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Athelstan_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Athelstan of England">King Athelstan</a> again strengthened the walls around 928, and at the same time drove out the remaining <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britons</a> from the city.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS23_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS23-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> (It is uncertain, though, whether they had lived in the city continuously since the Roman period or returned from the countryside when Alfred strengthened its defences.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>) According to <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a>, they were sent beyond the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a>, which was fixed as the boundary of Devon. (This may, however, have served as a territorial boundary within the former kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dumnonia" title="Dumnonia">Dumnonia</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>) Other references suggest that the British simply moved to what is now the <span class="nowrap">St. David\'s</span> area, not far outside Exeter\'s walls. The quarter vacated by the Britons was apparently adapted as "the <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earl</a>\'s burh" and was still named Irlesberi in the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS23_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS23-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In 1001, the Danes again failed to get into the city, but they were able to plunder it in 1003 because they were let in, for unknown reasons, by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Reeve_(England)" title="Reeve (England)">reeve</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Emma of Normandy</a>, who had been given the city as part of her dowry on her marriage to <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="\xc3\x86thelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred the Unready</a> the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS23_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS23-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse,_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg/170px-Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg/255px-Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg/340px-Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rougemont_Castle_Gatehouse,_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_333534.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The gatehouse of <a href="/wiki/Rougemont_Castle" title="Rougemont Castle">Rougemont Castle</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Two years after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest">Norman conquest of England</a>, Exeter rebelled against <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conquerer" class="mw-redirect" title="William the Conquerer">King William</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gytha_Thorkelsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Gytha Thorkelsd\xc3\xb3ttir">Gytha Thorkelsd\xc3\xb3ttir</a>, the mother of the slain <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">King Harold</a>, was living in the city at the time, and William promptly marched west and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Exeter_(1068)" title="Siege of Exeter (1068)">initiated a siege</a>. After 18 days, William accepted the city\'s honourable surrender, swearing an oath not to harm the city or increase its <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">ancient tribute</a>. However, William quickly arranged for the building of <a href="/wiki/Rougemont_Castle" title="Rougemont Castle">Rougemont Castle</a> to strengthen Norman control over the area. Properties owned by Saxon landlords were transferred into Norman hands and, on the death of <a href="/wiki/Leofric_(bishop)" title="Leofric (bishop)">Bishop Leofric</a> in 1072, the Norman <a href="/wiki/Osbern_FitzOsbern" title="Osbern FitzOsbern">Osbern FitzOsbern</a> was appointed his successor.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1136, early in <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">the Anarchy</a>, Rougemont Castle was held against <a href="/wiki/Stephen_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen of England">King Stephen</a> by <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_de_Redvers" class="mw-redirect" title="Baldwin de Redvers">Baldwin de Redvers</a>. Redvers submitted only after a three-month siege, not when the three wells in the castle ran dry, but only after the exhaustion of the large supplies of wine that the garrison was using for drinking, baking, cooking, and putting out fires set by the besiegers.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> During the siege, King Stephen built an earthen fortification at the site now known (erroneously) as Danes Castle.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg/220px-Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg/330px-Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg/440px-Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3552" data-file-height="2368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter_medieval_bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of the medieval Exe Bridge, built around 1200<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The city held a weekly market for the benefit of its citizens from at least 1213, and by 1281 Exeter was the only town in the south west to have three market days per week. There are also records of seven annual fairs, the earliest of which dates from 1130, and all of which continued until at least the early 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">expulsion</a> of the Jews of England in 1290, Exeter was home to England\'s most westerly Jewish community.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the high medieval period, both the cathedral clergy and the citizens enjoyed access to sophisticated aqueduct systems which brought pure drinking water into the city from springs in the neighbouring parish of St Sidwell\'s. For part of their length, these aqueducts were conveyed through a remarkable network of subterranean tunnels, or underground passages, which survive largely intact and which may still be visited today.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter,_1563.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Exeter%2C_1563.jpg/220px-Exeter%2C_1563.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Exeter%2C_1563.jpg/330px-Exeter%2C_1563.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Exeter%2C_1563.jpg/440px-Exeter%2C_1563.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter,_1563.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of Exeter in 1563, entitled <i>Civitas Exoniae (vulgo Excester) urbs primaria in comitatu Devoniae</i></div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Tudor and Stuart eras</dt></dl>\n<p>In 1537, the city was made a <a href="/wiki/County_corporate" title="County corporate">county corporate</a>. In 1549, the city successfully withstood a month-long siege by the so-called <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book rebels</a>: Devon and Cornish folk who had been infuriated by the radical religious policies of King Edward VI. The insurgents occupied the suburbs of Exeter, burnt down two of the city gates and attempted to undermine the city walls, but were eventually forced to abandon the siege after they had been worsted in a series of bloody battles with the king\xe2\x80\x99s army. A number of rebels were executed in the immediate aftermath of the siege.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Livery_Dole" title="Livery Dole">Livery Dole</a> <a href="/wiki/Almshouse" title="Almshouse">almshouses</a> and chapel at <a href="/wiki/Heavitree" title="Heavitree">Heavitree</a> were founded in March 1591 and finished in 1594.\n</p><p>The city\'s motto, <i><a href="/wiki/Semper_fidelis" title="Semper fidelis">Semper fidelis</a></i>, is traditionally held to have been suggested by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>, in acknowledgement of the city\'s contribution of ships to help defeat the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> in 1588;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> however its first documented use is in 1660. Schools in Exeter teach that the motto was bestowed by Charles II in 1660 at the Restoration due to Exeter\'s role in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>.\n</p><p>When in 1638 Reverend John Wheelwright was exiled from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a> and subsequently established a community on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Squamscott_River" title="Squamscott River">Squamscott River</a>, he named the region <a href="/wiki/Exeter,_New_Hampshire" title="Exeter, New Hampshire">Exeter</a> after its Devonian counterpart. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolution</a> it became the capital of <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Exeter was secured for Parliament at the beginning of the English Civil War, and its defences very much strengthened, but in September 1643 it was captured by the Cornish Royalist Army led by Prince Maurice. Thereafter, the city remained firmly under the king\xe2\x80\x99s control until near the end of the war, being one of the final Royalist cities to fall into Parliamentarian hands.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The surrender of Exeter was negotiated in April 1646 at <a href="/wiki/Poltimore_House" title="Poltimore House">Poltimore House</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fairfax" title="Thomas Fairfax">Thomas Fairfax</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> During this period, Exeter was an economically powerful city, with a strong trade of <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>. This was partly due to the surrounding area which was "more fertile and better inhabited than that passed over the preceding day" according to Count <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Magalotti" title="Lorenzo Magalotti">Lorenzo Magalotti</a> who visited the city when he was 26 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Magalotti writes of over thirty thousand people being employed in the county of Devon as part of the wool and cloth industries, merchandise that was sold to "the West Indies, Spain, France and Italy".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Celia Fiennes also visited Exeter during this period, in the early 1700s. She remarked on the "vast trade" and "incredible quantity" in Exeter, recording that "it turns the most money in a week of anything in England", between \xc2\xa310,000 and \xc2\xa315,000.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Georgian and Victorian eras</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg/220px-Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg/330px-Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg/440px-Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4663" data-file-height="3030" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roque%27s_Map_of_Exeter_1744.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Rocque" title="John Rocque">John Rocque</a>\'s 1744 map of Exeter</div></div></div>\n<p>Early in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, Exeter\'s industry developed on the basis of locally available agricultural products and, since the city\'s location on a fast-flowing river gave it ready access to <a href="/wiki/Water_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Water power">water power</a>, an early industrial site developed on drained marshland to the west of the city, at <a href="/wiki/Exe_Island" title="Exe Island">Exe Island</a>. However, when <a href="/wiki/Steam_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam power">steam power</a> replaced water in the 19th century, Exeter was too far from sources of coal (or iron) to develop further. As a result, the city declined in relative importance, and was spared the rapid 19th century development that changed many historic European cities. Extensive canal redevelopments during this period further expanded Exeter\'s economy, with "vessels of 15 to 16 tons burthen [bringing] up goods and merchandise from <a href="/wiki/Topsham,_Devon" title="Topsham, Devon">Topsham</a> to the City <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Quay" title="Exeter Quay">Quay</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 1778 a new bridge across the Exe was opened to replace the old medieval bridge. Built at a cost of \xc2\xa330,000, it had three arches and was built of stone.<sup id="cite_ref-exebridges_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exebridges-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg/220px-Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg/330px-Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg/440px-Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas-Shapter-HistoryOfCholeraInExeter1832-map.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frontispiece to Shapter\'s "History of the Cholera in Exeter in 1832"</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1832, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, which had been erupting all across Europe, reached Exeter. The only known documentation of this event was written by Dr <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Shapter" title="Thomas Shapter">Thomas Shapter</a>, one of the medical doctors present during the epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first railway to arrive in Exeter was the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_and_Exeter_Railway" title="Bristol and Exeter Railway">Bristol and Exeter Railway</a> that opened a station at <a href="/wiki/Exeter_St_Davids_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Exeter St Davids railway station">St Davids</a> on the western edge in 1844. The <a href="/wiki/South_Devon_Railway_Company" title="South Devon Railway Company">South Devon Railway Company</a> extended the line westwards to <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>, opening their own smaller station at <a href="/wiki/Exeter_St_Thomas_railway_station" title="Exeter St Thomas railway station">St Thomas</a>, above Cowick Street. A more central railway station, that at <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Central_railway_station" title="Exeter Central railway station">Queen Street</a>, was opened by the <a href="/wiki/London_and_South_Western_Railway" title="London and South Western Railway">London and South Western Railway</a> in 1860 when it opened its alternative route to London. Butchers <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Maunder" title="Lloyd Maunder">Lloyd Maunder</a> moved to their present base in 1915, to gain better access to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a> for transportation of <a href="/wiki/Meat_processing" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat processing">meat products</a> to London.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:High_Street,_Exeter,_England,_ca._1895.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg/220px-High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg/330px-High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg/440px-High_Street%2C_Exeter%2C_England%2C_ca._1895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3524" data-file-height="2564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:High_Street,_Exeter,_England,_ca._1895.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The High Street ca. 1895</div></div></div>\n<p>The first electricity in Exeter was provided by the Exeter Electric Light Company, which was formed at the end of the 1880s, but it was <a href="/wiki/Municipalization" title="Municipalization">municipalised</a> in 1896 and became the City of Exeter Electricity Company.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Exeter_tram_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Exeter tram system">horse-drawn trams in Exeter</a> were introduced in 1882 with 3 lines radiating from the city\'s East Gate. One line went to St David\'s station via New North Road, the Obelisk (where the Clock Tower now stands) and St David\'s Hill. The second line went out along Heavitree Road to Livery Dole and the third went to Mount Pleasant along Sidwell Street. There was a depot off New North Road.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamp_standard_and_ferry,_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg/220px-Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg/330px-Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg/440px-Lamp_standard_and_ferry%2C_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamp_standard_and_ferry,_Exeter_Quay_-_geograph.org.uk_-_836757.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lamp standard from the 1905 Exe bridge, installed at <a href="/wiki/Butts_Ferry" title="Butts Ferry">Butts Ferry</a> on <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Quayside" class="mw-redirect" title="Exeter Quayside">Exeter Quayside</a> in 1983</div></div></div>\n<p>On 29 March 1905 a new bridge across the Exe was opened replacing the former Georgian bridge. Made of <a href="/wiki/Cast-iron" class="mw-redirect" title="Cast-iron">cast-iron</a> and steel with a <a href="/wiki/Truss_arch_bridge" title="Truss arch bridge">three hinged arch</a> design, it cost \xc2\xa325,000 and was designed by <a href="/wiki/John_Wolfe-Barry" class="mw-redirect" title="John Wolfe-Barry">Sir John Wolfe Barry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-exebridges_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exebridges-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Also in 1905 <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Corporation_Tramways" class="mw-redirect" title="Exeter Corporation Tramways">electric trams</a> replaced the horse trams<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> with a new route which passed along the High Street, down Fore Street and over the new Exe Bridge. Once over the Exe the line divided, with one route along Alphington Road and another along Cowick Street. The line to St David\'s Station travelled along Queen Street instead of along New North Road and the line to Heavitree was extended.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> On 17 March 1917, a tram went out of control going down Fore Street, hit a horse-drawn wagon, then overturned on Exe Bridge and one female passenger was killed.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> By the 1920s, there were problems with congestion caused by the trams, a need for expensive track renewal work and the slow speed of the trams in Exeter\'s narrow streets. After much discussion the council decided to replace the tram service with double-decker buses and the last tram ran on 19 August 1931. The only remaining Exeter tram in service is car 19, now at the <a href="/wiki/Seaton_Tramway" title="Seaton Tramway">Seaton Tramway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Exeter was bombed by the German Luftwaffe in the Second World War, when a total of 18 raids between 1940 and 1942 flattened much of the city centre. \nBetween April 1941 and April 1943 Exeter was defended from enemy bombers by the Polish 307 Squadron, night-fighters nicknamed the \'Lw\xc3\xb3w Eagle Owls\' who were based at Exeter Airport. \nThe city of Lw\xc3\xb3w shared the same motto as the city of Exeter - \'Semper Fidelis\'.\n</p><p>In April and May 1942, as part of the Baedeker Blitz and specifically in response to the RAF bombing of L\xc3\xbcbeck and Rostock, 40 acres (16 ha) of the city, particularly adjacent to its central High Street and Sidwell Street, were levelled by incendiary bombing. Many historic buildings in the heart of the city were destroyed and others, including the Cathedral, were damaged. On the night of 4th May the only hope that Exeter had to avoid total destruction was the heavily outnumbered Polish 307 Squadron which had four available aircraft against the forty German Junkers-88 bombers. During the next 75 minutes the squadron prevented four German bombers releasing their load of bombs on Exeter. The squadron suffered no casualties in the process. \n156 people were killed in the attacks,[51]however a lot more people in Exeter would have been killed, and the effect on the city much greater had it not been for the \xe2\x80\x98Lw\xc3\xb3w Eagle Owls\xe2\x80\x99.\n</p><p>To demonstrate British-Polish cooperation and the friendship that had formed between 307 Squadron and Exeter, the squadron presented the city with a Polish flag on 15 November 1942 (the first British city to have had that honour) outside Exeter Cathedral.\n</p><p>Since 2012 a Polish flag is raised over the city\'s Guildhall on 15 November in honor of 307 Squadron, the day is now known as \'307 Squadron Day\' in Exeter.\nOn 15 November 2017 a plaque in memory of the squadron was unveiled in the St James Chapel of Exeter Cathedral by the Polish Ambassador His Excellency Arkady Rzegocki.\n</p><p>Large areas of the city centre were rebuilt in the 1950s, when little attempt was made to preserve Exeter\'s heritage. Damaged buildings were generally demolished rather than restored, and the street plan was altered in an attempt to improve traffic circulation. Former landmarks such as St. Lawrence and the College of the Vicars Choral disappeared. The modern architecture stands in sharp contrast to the red sandstone of buildings that survived the Blitz.<sup id="cite_ref-payne_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-payne-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg/220px-Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg/330px-Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg/440px-Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exe_Flood_Relief_Channel_-_geograph.org.uk_-_695255.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exe Flood Relief Channel built after the floods of 1960</div></div></div>\n<p>On 27 October 1960, following very heavy rain, the Exe overflowed and flooded large areas of Exeter including Exwick, St Thomas and Alphington. The water rose as high as 2 metres above ground level in places and 150 employees of the local firm Beach Bros were trapped for nine hours. 2,500 properties were flooded. Later the same year on 3 December the river levels rose again, flooding 1,200 properties. These floods led to the construction of new <a href="/wiki/Flood_defences" class="mw-redirect" title="Flood defences">flood defences</a> for Exeter. Work began in 1965, took 12 years to complete and cost \xc2\xa38 million. The defences included three flood relief channels, and were complemented by the construction of two new concrete bridges (built in 1969 and 1972) to replace the old Exe Bridge which had obstructed the flow of the river and made the flooding worse.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Princesshay" title="Princesshay">Princesshay</a> shopping centre adjoining the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Close,_Exeter" title="Cathedral Close, Exeter">Cathedral Close</a> and the High Street was redeveloped between 2005 and 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> despite some local opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It incorporates 123 varied residential units.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To enable people with limited mobility to enjoy the city, Exeter Community Transport Association provides manual and powered wheelchairs and scooters (called Shopmobility) for use by anyone suffering from short or long-term mobility impairment to access to the city centre and shopping facilities, events and meetings with friends and company.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2008 there was an <a href="/wiki/22_May_2008_Exeter_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="22 May 2008 Exeter bombing">attempted terrorist attack</a> on the Giraffe cafe in Princesshay, but the bomber was the only one injured.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A \xc2\xa330 million improvement scheme for the flood defences was approved in March 2015. The plans involve the removal of <a href="/wiki/Check_dam" title="Check dam">check weirs</a> and a deeper, "meandering stream" in the centre of the drainage channels to improve flow. The plans followed a study by the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a> that revealed weaknesses in the current defences.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Community_currency" title="Community currency">community currency</a> for the city, the <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Pound" class="mw-redirect" title="Exeter Pound">Exeter Pound</a>, was introduced in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A serious fire broke out in buildings in central Exeter on 28 October 2016. The fire largely destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Clarence_Hotel" title="Royal Clarence Hotel">Royal Clarence Hotel</a>, considered the first venue in England to call itself a hotel.  Other historic buildings, including 18 Cathedral Yard,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> were also destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> All of the damaged buildings are currently undergoing a major restoration. On 22 February 2017 archaeologists involved in the restoration and rebuilding of The Royal Clarence Hotel and 18 Cathedral Yard unearthed medieval pictures, including one of a peacock. The work which had taken three months to complete the process of the deconstruction of the buildings, with every effort being made to save as many historical features as possible. It will take a total of 21 months to restore the fa\xc3\xa7ade external of the buildings and a modern design planned for their interiors of the hotel and 18 Cathedral Yard.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> On 25 July 2017 official restoration plans were officially  unveiled for the exterior frontal fa\xc3\xa7ade of the building. Further plans were still underway for the interior of the hotel the restoration is being undertaken by construction consultants Thomasons, in partnership with <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> architects Buttress, <a href="/wiki/Historic_England" title="Historic England">Historic England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exeter_City_Council" title="Exeter City Council">Exeter City Council</a>. The rebuild is expected to be completed in 18 months time with a scheduled reopening of the hotel in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg/220px-Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg/330px-Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg/440px-Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter_Combined_Court_Centre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Exeter Crown and County Courts Building.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civic_Centre,_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg/170px-Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg/255px-Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg/340px-Civic_Centre%2C_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civic_Centre,_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_271614.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exeter Civic Centre</div></div></div>\n<p>Exeter is in two parliamentary constituencies, the majority of the city is in the <a href="/wiki/Exeter_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Exeter (UK Parliament constituency)">Exeter constituency</a> but two wards (St Loyes and Topsham) are in <a href="/wiki/East_Devon_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Devon (UK Parliament constituency)">East Devon</a>. Exeter itself is relatively marginal, and since World War II its Member of Parliament has usually been drawn from the governing party, though the Exeter seat is becoming increasingly a Labour stronghold.  The Exeter MP is <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bradshaw" title="Ben Bradshaw">Ben Bradshaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Swire" title="Hugo Swire">Hugo Swire</a> represents East Devon. Exeter is part of the <a href="/wiki/South_West_England_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="South West England (European Parliament constituency)">South West England European constituency</a>, which elects 6 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a>.\n</p><p>Exeter\'s <a href="/wiki/City_council" title="City council">city council</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Non-metropolitan_district" title="Non-metropolitan district">district</a> authority, and shares responsibility for local government with the <a href="/wiki/Devon_County_Council" title="Devon County Council">Devon County Council</a>. In May 2012 Labour became the majority party on the council. <a href="/wiki/Exeter_City_Council" title="Exeter City Council">Exeter City Council</a>\'s bid for the city to become a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Unitary Authority">Unitary Authority</a> was initially approved by ministers in February 2010. A judicial review was called by Devon County Council and the Court held that the Minister had acted unlawfully in granting Unitary status to Exeter at the same time, however, following the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a> the <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">new coalition government</a> announced in May 2010 that the reorganisation would be blocked.<sup id="cite_ref-coalition_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coalition-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From Saxon times, it was in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred (county subdivision)">hundred</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wonford_(hundred)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wonford (hundred)">Wonford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-genuki-devonhundreds_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genuki-devonhundreds-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Exeter has had a <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Exeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayor of Exeter">mayor</a> since at least 1207 and until 2002, the city was the oldest \'Right Worshipful\' Mayoralty in England. As part of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Golden Jubilee of Elizabeth II">Golden Jubilee of Elizabeth II</a> Exeter was chosen to receive the title of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mayor">Lord Mayor</a>. Councillor Granville Baldwin became the first Lord Mayor of Exeter on 1 May 2002 when <a href="/wiki/Letters_Patent" class="mw-redirect" title="Letters Patent">Letters Patent</a> were awarded to the city during a visit by the Queen.\nThe Lord Mayor is elected each year from amongst the 39 Exeter city <a href="/wiki/Councillor" title="Councillor">councillors</a> and is non-political for the term of office.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_services">Public services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Public services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Policing in Exeter is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Devon_and_Cornwall_Constabulary" class="mw-redirect" title="Devon and Cornwall Constabulary">Devon and Cornwall Constabulary</a> who have their headquarters at Middlemoor in the east of the city.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fire_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Fire service">fire service</a> is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Devon_and_Somerset_Fire_and_Rescue_Service" title="Devon and Somerset Fire and Rescue Service">Devon and Somerset Fire and Rescue Service</a>, which is headquartered at Clyst St. George near Exeter. It has two fire stations located at Danes Castle and Middlemoor.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Devon_and_Exeter_NHS_Foundation_Trust" title="Royal Devon and Exeter NHS Foundation Trust">Royal Devon and Exeter NHS Foundation Trust</a> has a large hospital located to the south east of the city centre. Ambulance services in Exeter are provided by <a href="/wiki/South_Western_Ambulance_Service" title="South Western Ambulance Service">South Western Ambulance Service</a> NHS Trust. The West Trust Divisional HQ and 999 control is in Exeter which provides cover for <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, Cornwall, <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_in_Exeter" title="List of places in Exeter">List of places in Exeter</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg/220px-River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg/330px-River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg/440px-River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Exe_at_Exeter_-_geograph.org.uk_-_258229.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The River Exe</div></div></div>\n<p>The city of Exeter was established on the eastern bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">River Exe</a> on a ridge of land backed by a steep hill. It is at this point that the Exe, having just been joined by the <a href="/wiki/River_Creedy" title="River Creedy">River Creedy</a>, opens onto a wide <a href="/wiki/Flood_plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Flood plain">flood plain</a> and estuary which results in quite common flooding. Historically this was the <a href="/wiki/Lowest_bridging_point" title="Lowest bridging point">lowest bridging point</a> of the River Exe which was tidal and navigable up to the city until the construction of weirs later in its history. This combined with the easily defensible higher ground of the ridge made the current location of the city a natural choice for settlement and trade. In <a href="/wiki/George_Oliver_(historian)" title="George Oliver (historian)">George Oliver</a>\'s <i>The History of the City of Exeter</i>, it is noted that the most likely reasons for the original settling of what would become modern Exeter was the "fertility of the surrounding countryside" and the area\'s "beautiful and commanding elevation [and] its rapid and navigable river".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\nIts woodland would also have been ideal for natural resources and hunting.\n</p><p>Exeter sits predominantly on sandstone and conglomerate geology, although the structure of the surrounding areas is varied.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\nThe topography of the ridge which forms the backbone of the city includes a <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_plug" title="Volcanic plug">volcanic plug</a>, on which the <a href="/wiki/Rougemont_Castle" title="Rougemont Castle">Rougemont Castle</a> is situated. The Cathedral is located on the edge of this ridge and is therefore visible for a considerable distance.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>Exeter is 80 miles (130&#160;km) west-southwest of <a href="/wiki/Salisbury" title="Salisbury">Salisbury</a>, 158 miles (254&#160;km) west-southwest of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, 18 miles (29&#160;km) north of <a href="/wiki/Torquay" title="Torquay">Torquay</a>, 36 miles (58&#160;km) northeast of Plymouth and 74 miles (119&#160;km) east-northeast of <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Exeter has mild wet winters and warm changeable summers with hot and cooler rainy spells. Temperatures do not vary much throughout the year. The hottest month is July with an average high of 21.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C (71.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F), and the coldest month is January with an average high of 8.8&#160;\xc2\xb0C (47.8&#160;\xc2\xb0F). October is the wettest month with 88.9 millimetres (3.50&#160;in) of rain. Because of shelter from <a href="/wiki/Dartmoor" title="Dartmoor">Dartmoor</a>, Exeter is more frost prone than areas to the southwest, such as <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>. It is also drier, and warmer in the summer for the same reason. The highest recorded temperature in Exeter stands at 33.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C (92.3&#160;\xc2\xb0F)<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> recorded in June 1976, whilst the lowest recorded temperature in Exeter is \xe2\x88\x9216.4&#160;\xc2\xb0C (2.5&#160;\xc2\xb0F)<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> recorded in December 2010.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; Meanwhile, the Chinese and <i>Other Asian</i> had the largest increases (429% and 434% respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-ethnicity_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnicity-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> This excludes the two new ethnic groups added to the 2011 census of Gypsy or Irish Traveller and Arab. 113,507. The Exeter USD does not include the outlying suburb of <a href="/wiki/Topsham,_Devon" title="Topsham, Devon">Topsham</a>, which while it is administratively part of the city, it is often considered a separate individual settlement as well as the fact its excluded from the city\'s constituency.  \n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Exeter compared 2011</th>\n<th>Exeter USD</th>\n<th>Exeter City\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>White British</td>\n<td>88.1%</td>\n<td>88.3%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Asian</td>\n<td>4.0%</td>\n<td>3.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Black</td>\n<td>0.6%</td>\n<td>0.5%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011, 11.9% of the population of the Exeter USD (urban subdivision) were non white British, compared with 11.7% for the actual city and surrounding borough of Exeter.\n</p><p>In 2009, Exeter City was 89.1% <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">White British</a>, compared with 88.3% in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Exeter Urban Area had a population of 124,079<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> in 2014, compared with 124,328 for the city and borough of Exeter. While the Exeter Metropolitan Area had a population of 467,257 in the same year and includes Exeter along with <a href="/wiki/Teignbridge" title="Teignbridge">Teignbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mid_Devon" title="Mid Devon">Mid Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Devon" title="East Devon">East Devon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Out of all the Devon districts, Exeter receives the largest number of commuters from East Devon, followed by Teignbridge. Most of the city\'s ethnic minority population live in the central, north western and eastern suburbs of the city. Outlying areas such as <a href="/wiki/Pinhoe" title="Pinhoe">Pinhoe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cowick,_Devon" title="Cowick, Devon">Cowick</a> and the expensive suburb of <a href="/wiki/Topsham,_Devon" title="Topsham, Devon">Topsham</a> are all 95% White British as of 2011.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG/220px-Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG/330px-Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG/440px-Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_from_Princesshay_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Princesshay Shopping Centre with Exeter Cathedral in the background</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter,_Devon,_England,_UK,_October_1943_D16650.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg/220px-A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg/330px-A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg/440px-A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter%2C_Devon%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_October_1943_D16650.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="549" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Middle_East_Soldier_Revisits_Britain-_Life_in_Wartime_Exeter,_Devon,_England,_UK,_October_1943_D16650.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Higher Market, Exeter, in 1943; at this time the larger firms such as <a href="/wiki/Mac_Fisheries" title="Mac Fisheries">Mac Fisheries</a> had joined the smaller shopkeepers\' stalls in the market, which, before the war, along with neighbouring Goldsmith Street, was earmarked for demolition; a new Civic Centre was to be built on the site</div></div></div> \n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Met_Office" title="Met Office">Met Office</a>, the main weather forecasting organisation for the United Kingdom and one of the most significant in the world, relocated from <a href="/wiki/Bracknell" title="Bracknell">Bracknell</a> in Berkshire to Exeter in early 2004. It is one of the largest employers in the area (together with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devon_County_Council" title="Devon County Council">Devon County Council</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Devon_and_Exeter_NHS_Foundation_Trust" title="Royal Devon and Exeter NHS Foundation Trust">Royal Devon and Exeter NHS Foundation Trust</a>).\n</p><p>Around 35,000 people commute into Exeter on a daily basis, from nearby surrounding towns.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Exeter provides services, employment and shopping for local residents within the city limits and also from nearby towns in <a href="/wiki/Teignbridge" title="Teignbridge">Teignbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mid_Devon" title="Mid Devon">Mid Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Devon" title="East Devon">East Devon</a>, together sometimes known as the Exeter &amp; Heart of Devon area (EHOD). Exeter therefore provides for the EHOD area population of 457,400.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Exeter has been identified among the top ten most profitable locations for a business to be based.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The city centre provides substantial shopping facilities. The High Street is mainly devoted to branches of national chains: a <a href="/wiki/New_Economics_Foundation" title="New Economics Foundation">NEF</a> survey in 2005 rated Exeter as the worst example of a <a href="/wiki/Clone_town" title="Clone town">clone town</a> in the UK, with only a single independent store in the city\'s High Street, and less diversity (in terms of different categories of shop) than any other town surveyed. In 2010, a similar survey reported the city was still the worst clone town.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Three significant shopping areas that connect to the High Street provide a somewhat more varied menu. <a href="/wiki/Princesshay" title="Princesshay">Princesshay</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> retail area connecting to the south side of the High Street was home to a number of independent stores prior to redevelopment in 2007, but is now also largely occupied by national chains. It is still intended that a number of the new units will be let to local independent stores.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> On the other side of the High Street, the partly undercover Guildhall Shopping Centre houses a mixture of national and more regional shops, and connects to the wholly enclosed Harlequins Centre where smaller businesses predominate. Smaller streets off the High Street such as Gandy Street also offer a range of independent shops.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>On 26 June 2004, Exeter was granted <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade City">Fairtrade City</a> status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Although Exeter contains a number of tourist attractions, the city is not dominated by tourism, with only 7% of employment dependent on tourism compared with 13% for Devon as a whole (2005 figures).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are also plans to build on land in the Teignbridge and East Devon areas, which border Exeter\'s boundaries.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Grade_I_listed_buildings_in_Exeter" title="Grade I listed buildings in Exeter">Grade I listed buildings in Exeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grade_II*_listed_buildings_in_Exeter" title="Grade II* listed buildings in Exeter">Grade II* listed buildings in Exeter</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:908px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exeter_panorama_1.jpg" class="image" title="A panorama of the city taken from the roof of the north tower of the cathedral"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Exeter_panorama_1.jpg/900px-Exeter_panorama_1.jpg" width="900" height="314"
